        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b05033"><meta property="og:title" content="Two-Fold Anisotropy Governs Morphological Evolution and Stress Generation in Sodiated Black Phosphorus for Sodium Ion Batteries"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b05033/asset/images/acs.nanolett.6b05033.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="Phosphorus represents a promising anode material for sodium ion batteries owing to its extremely high theoretical capacity. Recent in situ transmission electron microscopy studies evidenced anisotropic swelling in sodiated black phosphorus, which may find an origin from the two intrinsic anisotropic properties inherent to the layered structure of black phosphorus: sodium diffusional directionality and insertion strain anisotropy. To understand the morphological evolution and stress generation in sodiated black phosphorus, we develop a chemo-mechanical model by incorporating the intrinsic anisotropic properties into the large elasto-plastic deformation. Our modeling results reveal that the apparent morphological evolution in sodiated black phosphorus is critically controlled by the coupled effect of the two intrinsic anisotropic properties. In particular, sodium diffusional directionality generates sharp interphases along the [010] and [001] directions, which constrain anisotropic development of the insertion strain. The coupled effect renders distinctive stress-generation and fracture mechanisms when sodiation starts from different crystal facets. In addition to providing a powerful modeling framework for sodiation and lithiation of layered structures, our findings shed significant light on the sodiation-induced chemo-mechanical degradation of black phosphorus as a promising anode for the next-generation sodium ion batteries."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b05033/asset/images/acs.nanolett.6b05033.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/nwyw~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="90f6e2a85b9e775c-LHR"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="90f6e2a85b9e775c-LHR">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: '0dd0a411-2976-433f-8819-cb10ec5d342a'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="90f6e2a85b9e775c-LHR">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'nano_letters';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/nano_letters/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/nano_letters/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

    var serialCode = "nalefd";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Two-Fold Anisotropy Governs Morphological Evolution and Stress Generation in Sodiated Black Phosphorus for Sodium Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b05033", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Two-Fold Anisotropy Governs Morphological Evolution and Stress Generation in Sodiated Black Phosphorus for Sodium Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b05033", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Two-Fold Anisotropy Governs Morphological Evolution and Stress Generation in Sodiated Black Phosphorus for Sodium Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b05033", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Two-Fold Anisotropy Governs Morphological Evolution and Stress Generation in Sodiated Black Phosphorus for Sodium Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b05033", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Two-Fold Anisotropy Governs Morphological Evolution and Stress Generation in Sodiated Black Phosphorus for Sodium Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b05033", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Two-Fold Anisotropy Governs Morphological Evolution and Stress Generation in Sodiated Black Phosphorus for Sodium Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b05033", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Two-Fold Anisotropy Governs Morphological Evolution and Stress Generation in Sodiated Black Phosphorus for Sodium Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b05033", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Two-Fold Anisotropy Governs Morphological Evolution and Stress Generation in Sodiated Black Phosphorus for Sodium Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b05033", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Two-Fold Anisotropy Governs Morphological Evolution and Stress Generation in Sodiated Black Phosphorus for Sodium Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b05033", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

Empty dropzone --></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="f_tgr1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b05033/asset/images/medium/nl-2016-05033m_0007.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b05033/asset/images/large/nl-2016-05033m_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b05033&amp;id=f_tgr1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Phosphorus represents a promising anode material for sodium ion batteries owing to its extremely high theoretical capacity. Recent in situ transmission electron microscopy studies evidenced anisotropic swelling in sodiated black phosphorus, which may find an origin from the two intrinsic anisotropic properties inherent to the layered structure of black phosphorus: sodium diffusional directionality and insertion strain anisotropy. To understand the morphological evolution and stress generation in sodiated black phosphorus, we develop a chemo-mechanical model by incorporating the intrinsic anisotropic properties into the large elasto-plastic deformation. Our modeling results reveal that the apparent morphological evolution in sodiated black phosphorus is critically controlled by the coupled effect of the two intrinsic anisotropic properties. In particular, sodium diffusional directionality generates sharp interphases along the [010] and [001] directions, which constrain anisotropic development of the insertion strain. The coupled effect renders distinctive stress-generation and fracture mechanisms when sodiation starts from different crystal facets. In addition to providing a powerful modeling framework for sodiation and lithiation of layered structures, our findings shed significant light on the sodiation-induced chemo-mechanical degradation of black phosphorus as a promising anode for the next-generation sodium ion batteries.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.

href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D2299%26pageCount%3D8%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DTianwu%2BChen%252C%2BPeng%2BZhao%252C%2BXu%2BGuo%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D17%26issueNum%3D4%26contentID%3Dacs.nanolett.6b05033%26title%3DTwo-Fold%2BAnisotropy%2BGoverns%2BMorphological%2BEvolution%2Band%2BStress%2BGeneration%2Bin%2BSodiated%2BBlack%2BPhosphorus%2Bfor%2BSodium%2BIon%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D8%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1530-6984%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Dnalefd%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D2306%26publicationDate%3DApril%2B2017" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright Â© 2017 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290830&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Diffusion" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Diffusion" class="article__tags__link">Diffusion</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290998&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Magnetic properties" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Magnetic%20properties" class="article__tags__link">Magnetic properties</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292105&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Phosphorus" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Phosphorus" class="article__tags__link">Phosphorus</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291251&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Sodium" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Sodium" class="article__tags__link">Sodium</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292548&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Stress" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Stress" class="article__tags__link">Stress</a></li></ul></div></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium+Ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_Sodium%20ion%20batteries">Sodium ion batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Black+Phosphorus&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_black%20phosphorus">black phosphorus</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Anisotropic+Swelling&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_anisotropic%20swelling">anisotropic swelling</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Chemo-mechanical+Modeling&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_chemo-mechanical%20modeling">chemo-mechanical modeling</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Stress+Generation&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_stress%20generation">stress generation</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody"><p class="first">Cost-effective sodium (Na) ion batteries (NIBs) have recently drawn significant interests for their potential applications in future renewables-friendly, grid-scale energy storage. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2" aria-label="reference 1 and 2">(1, 2)</a> Though the similar chemistry enables accessible design strategies for NIBs parallel to lithium ion battery (LIB) technologies, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3 ref4'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3 ref4" aria-label="reference 3 and 4">(3, 4)</a> it has remained a challenge to identify high-performance electrode materials compatible for sodium ions. Graphite, the most popular anode used in commercial LIBs, cannot be directly used as anode for NIBs because of the larger size of Na ions than Li ions and correspondingly the higher intercalation barrier. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref5 ref6'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5 ref6" aria-label="reference 5 and 6">(5, 6)</a> Silicon (Si), one of the most promising next-generation anode materials for LIBs, is inert to sodium insertion at room temperature. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7" aria-label="reference 7">(7)</a> Phosphorus (P) and phosphorus-based compounds represent a promising new class of anode materials for NIBs <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8 ref9'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8 ref9" aria-label="reference 8 and 9">(8, 9)</a> for their much higher theoretical capacity (Na<sub>3</sub>P, â¼2600 mAh/g) than both carbon-based materials (e.g., hard carbon, â¼300 mAh/g) and intermetallic alloys (e.g., Sn and Sb, â¼600â900 mAh/g). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref10 ref11 ref12'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10 ref11 ref12" aria-label="reference 10 to 12">(10-12)</a> However, similar to intermetallic alloy anodes (e.g., Si and Ge) for LIBs, phosphorus undergoes huge volume change (â¼400%) during sodiation/desodiation, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref13'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13" aria-label="reference 13">(13)</a> leading to pulverization of the active materials, unstable growth of solid-electrolyte interphase (SEI), and fast capacity fading. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref14 ref15'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14 ref15" aria-label="reference 14 and 15">(14, 15)</a> In terms of its high capacity and large volume change, phosphorus in NIBs can be regarded as the counterpart of Si in LIBs. Despite rapid progress in understanding the lithiation-induced failure <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19 ref20 ref21 ref22 ref23 ref24 ref25 ref26 ref27 ref28 ref29 ref30'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19 ref20 ref21 ref22 ref23 ref24 ref25 ref26 ref27 ref28 ref29 ref30" aria-label="reference 16 to 30">(16-30)</a> and novel designs of Si anodes for LIBs, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref31 ref32 ref33 ref34 ref35 ref36 ref37'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31 ref32 ref33 ref34 ref35 ref36 ref37" aria-label="reference 31 to 37">(31-37)</a> the degradation mechanism of phosphorus during sodiation/desodiation cycles has remained largely unexplored.</p><p>Herein, we formulate a chemo-mechanical model to simulate morphological evolution and stress generation in black phosphorus during sodiation. The model couples sodium diffusion and anisotropic insertion strain with large elastic-plastic deformation in the three-dimensional (3D) setting. Our simulation results reveal that two intrinsic anisotropies, sodium diffusional directionality and insertion strain anisotropy, influence one another, and together they control the morphological evolution and stress generation in sodiated black phosphorus. Our mechanistic understanding provides guidance to mitigate degradation of the high-capacity anodes for NIBs.</p><p>Among the three main allotropes of phosphorus (white, red, and black), white phosphorus is generally excluded from the use as battery materials for its high reactivity and toxicity. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> It has been reported that amorphous red phosphorus has a charge capacity of 897 mAh/g and discharge capacity of 15 mAh/g, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref13'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13" aria-label="reference 13">(13)</a> showing irreversible sodium insertion. Closely resembling graphite, black phosphorus has the most interesting structure among these allotropic forms, and is better suited as an electrode material. With a layered structure, black phosphorus is more stable and has a higher electrical conductivity than red phosphorus. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref39'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a> Within the same layer, each phosphorus atom covalently binds with three neighbors, while van der Waals interactions operate between the layers. The interlayer spacing of black phosphorus (5.4 Ã) is much larger than that of graphite (3.4 Ã), suggesting that sodium ions can intercalate into black phosphorus much more easily than into graphite. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref40'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> Recent experimental studies have shown that black phosphorus exhibits a much higher charge (2035 mAh/g) and discharge (637 mAh/g) capacities than red phosphorus. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref13'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13" aria-label="reference 13">(13)</a> It has also been reported that a grapheneâphosphorene hybrid yields even higher capacity and more stable cyclability. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a></p><p>Electrochemical charging of black phosphorus involves a two-step sodiation process in series: intercalation followed by alloying. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> Different from the flat layers in graphite, the layers in black phosphorus are puckered, with the pucker channels aligned along the [100] direction, as schematically shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>. In the first step of sodiation (intercalation), sodium ions insert into black phosphorus along the pucker channels and fill out the interlayer space until a phase Na<sub>0.25</sub>P is formed. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38 ref40'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38 ref40" aria-label="reference 38 and 40">(38, 40)</a> The intercalation expands the interlayer spacing but preserves the layered structure of black phosphorus. In the second step of sodiation (alloying), further accumulated sodium breaks the PâP bonds, transforming the layered structure to amorphous sodium phosphide (Na<sub>3</sub>P) in the fully sodiated stage.</p><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b05033/asset/images/medium/nl-2016-05033m_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b05033/asset/images/large/nl-2016-05033m_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. Crystal structure of black phosphorus and its sodiation mechanism. (aâc) The 3D, front, and top view of black phosphorus, respectively. (d) In the first step of sodiation, sodium ions are intercalated into the interlayer space to form Na<sub>0.25</sub>P. (e) In the second step of sodiation, the phosphorus is alloyed to form Na<sub>3</sub>P. Blue particles, phosphorus; purple particles, sodium.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b05033/asset/images/large/nl-2016-05033m_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b05033&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p>Black phosphorus shows anisotropic swelling upon sodiation. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> The atomic mechanisms for the anisotropic swelling have not been explored, but possibly stem from the two intrinsic anisotropic properties: sodium diffusional directionality in black phosphorus and sodiation induced strain anisotropy. First, inherent to its pucker-layered structure, black phosphorus exhibits a âselective diffusional behaviorâ during the first step of intercalation. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref41'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41" aria-label="reference 41">(41)</a> As reported, regardless of the contacting geometry between the sodium source and the black phosphorus, sodium atoms preferably diffuse along the [100] direction. This is due to the much lower sodium diffusion barriers in the [100] direction than in the [010] and [001] directions. To be specific, the diffusion barrier is 0.18 eV in the [100] direction, while at least 0.76 eV in the other two directions. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref40'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> Structurally, the puckers function as fast diffusion channels. During the second step of sodiation, because the layered structure is gradually transformed into an amorphous compound as the sodium concentration increases, sodium diffusion becomes isotropic. Second, unlike the cubic crystalline structure of <i>c</i>-Si for which lithium insertion induces isotropic strain (known as the chemical strain), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref29 ref42'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29 ref42" aria-label="reference 29 and 42">(29, 42)</a> the orthotropically layered structure of black phosphorus endows anisotropic chemical strain, with much larger expansion along the [001] direction than other directions. The chemical strain anisotropy resembles that in layered graphene upon lithiation, where the expansion is predominant along the direction normal to the layers. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref26 ref43 ref44 ref45'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26 ref43 ref44 ref45" aria-label="reference 26 and 43 to 45">(26, 43-45)</a></p><p>A critical feature in lithiation of Si is the formation of sharp interphase that separates the Li-poor and Li-rich domains. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref30 ref46 ref47'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30 ref46 ref47" aria-label="reference 30, 46 and 47">(30, 46, 47)</a> Though high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (TEM) imaging is yet to be conducted to resolve the atomic structure of the interphases, the nature of the interphases may be rationalized from the orientation and phase-dependent sodiation kinetics. Sodiation is a process involving chemical reaction and atomic diffusion in parallel. During sodiation, the tilted bonds of the puckers break first; further sodium accumulation and invasion breaks the bonds within the plane of the layer, followed by solid-state amorphization. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38 ref40'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38 ref40" aria-label="reference 38 and 40">(38, 40)</a> Because the first step of sodiation preserves the pucker-layered structure, the diffusivities remain nearly unchanged therefore leading to a diffuse interphase in all the directions. In the second step of sodiation, behind the reaction front sodium fast diffuses toward the interphase in an isotropic manner due to the amorphized structure and the consequently much reduced diffusional barriers in [010] and [001] directions. However, limited by the low diffusivity in the partially sodiated black phosphorus (Na<sub>0.25</sub>P), the reaction front migrates very slowly along the [010] and [001] directions. This rate difference allows the Na-rich phase to easily catch up the reaction front and likely results in a sharp interphase along these two directions. However, along the [100] direction the comparable sodium diffusivity in front of and behind the reaction front renders a diffuse interphase. The diffuse interphase is also manifested by the gradual volume expansion along the [100] direction observed in experiments. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> This two-step sodiation process of black phosphorus is in clear contrast to the two-step lithiation of amorphous Si (<i>a</i>-Si), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref48 ref49 ref50'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref48 ref49 ref50" aria-label="reference 48 to 50">(48-50)</a> where <i>a</i>-Si is first lithiated to Li<sub>2.5</sub>Si by propagation of a sharp interphase and finally to Li<sub>3.75</sub>Si with an invisible diffuse interphase.</p><p>Our chemo-mechanical model incorporates the two-step sodiation and the 2-fold anisotropy, as well as the sharp and diffuse interphases. As the mechanical process is of a much shorter time scale, we assume that mechanical equilibrium is instantaneously met, while sodium transport is governed by the diffusion equation. In the first step of sodiation, sodium diffusivities are set to be orientation dependent with <i>D</i><sub class="stack">[100]</sub><sup class="stack">1</sup> = 1000<i>D</i><sub class="stack">[010]</sub><sup class="stack">1</sup> = 1000<i>D</i><sub class="stack">[001]</sub><sup class="stack">1</sup> to capture the feature and avoid the numerical divergence. Here the superscript denotes the sodiation step, while the subscript denotes the orientation. Note that the sodium diffusivities in [010] and [001] directions are set to be the same despite the different diffusional barriers because they are both negligibly small compared to that in the [100] direction. The diffusivities on the surfaces in the three directions are at least one order of magnitude larger than the ones in the same direction in the bulk, consistent with the much lower energy barriers for surface diffusion. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref40 ref41'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40 ref41" aria-label="reference 40 and 41">(40, 41)</a> In the second step of sodiation, sodium diffusivities are set to be nonlinearly dependent on sodium concentration along the [010] and [001] directions: <i>D</i><sub class="stack">[010]</sub><sup class="stack">2</sup> = <i>D</i><sub class="stack">[001]</sub><sup class="stack">2</sup> = <i>D</i><sub>0</sub>[1/(1 â <i>c</i>)â 2Î±<i>c</i>], <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref42 ref51'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42 ref51" aria-label="reference 42 and 51">(42, 51)</a> where <i>D</i><sub>0</sub> and Î± are tunable constants to control the interphase profile between the Na-rich and Na-poor phases and <i>c</i> is the sodium concentration. Along the [100] direction, the diffusivity remains unchanged from the first step. The cutoff value of the above nonlinear function is set to be <i>D</i><sub>cutoff</sub> = <i>D</i><sub class="stack">[100]</sub><sup class="stack">1</sup> = <i>D</i><sub class="stack">[100]</sub><sup class="stack">2</sup> to ensure isotropic diffusion when <i>c</i> approaches unity. In our simulations, the two steps are distinguished by setting different Dirichlet boundary conditions. In the present model, the sodium concentration, <i>c</i>, is normalized by the fully sodiated phase of Na<sub>3</sub>P, where <i>c</i> = 1 represents the Na<sub>3</sub>P phase and <i>c</i> = 0 represents the pristine black phosphorus phase. Therefore, <i>c</i> = 0.083 is set to be the surface boundary condition for the first step of sodiation in order to match the composition of Na<sub>0.25</sub>P, and <i>c</i> = 1 for the second step.</p><p>In the finite-strain framework, sodiation-induced deformation consists of the stretch rates and the spin rates. The total stretch rate is additive of the three components, the chemical (ÎµÌ<sub class="stack"><i>ij</i></sub><sup class="stack">c</sup>), elastic (ÎµÌ<sub class="stack"><i>ij</i></sub><sup class="stack">e</sup>), and plastic (ÎµÌ<sub class="stack"><i>ij</i></sub><sup class="stack">p</sup>) , ÎµÌ<sub><i>ij</i></sub> = ÎµÌ<sub class="stack"><i>ij</i></sub><sup class="stack">c</sup> + ÎµÌ<sub class="stack"><i>ij</i></sub><sup class="stack">e</sup> + ÎµÌ<sub class="stack"><i>ij</i></sub><sup class="stack">p</sup>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref42'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a> The chemical stretch rate is assumed to be proportional to the increment of the sodium concentration, ÎµÌ<sub class="stack"><i>ij</i></sub><sup class="stack">c</sup> = Î²<sub><i>ij</i></sub><i>Ä</i>. The diagonal tensor, Î²<sub><i>ij</i></sub>, represents the sodiation expansion coefficients in three different directions, yielding chemical strain anisotropy. We set Î²<sub>11</sub> = 0, Î²<sub>22</sub> = 0.92, Î²<sub>33</sub> = 1.6, and Î²<sub><i>ij</i></sub> = 0 for the other entries, where Î²<sub>11</sub>, Î²<sub>22</sub>, and Î²<sub>33</sub> are corresponding expansions in the [100], [010], and [001] directions, respectively. The elastic stretch rate, ÎµÌ<sub class="stack"><i>ij</i></sub><sup class="stack">e</sup>, obeys Hookeâs law with the stiffness tensor, <i>C</i><sub><i>ijkl</i></sub>, of the orthotropic crystals. As the crystalline black phosphorus evolves into the amorphous sodium phosphide during sodiation, the material changes from orthotropic to isotropic. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref52'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref52" aria-label="reference 52">(52)</a> Correspondingly, the stiffness tensor changes from nine independent materials constants for the unsodiated stage to only two for the fully sodiated stage (i.e., Youngâs modulus <i>E</i> and Poissonâs ratio Î½). For the intermediate states of charge, the stiffness tensor is assumed to be linearly dependent on sodium concentration, interpolated by these two extreme states. The values of the elastic parameters used in our model are listed in the <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b05033/suppl_file/nl6b05033_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>. The plastic stretch rate, ÎµÌ<sub class="stack"><i>ij</i></sub><sup class="stack">p</sup>, obeys the classic <i>J</i><sub>2</sub>-flow rule. Namely, plastic yielding occurs when the equivalent stress, Ï<sub>eq</sub> = (3<i>s</i><sub><i>ij</i></sub><i>s</i><sub><i>ij</i></sub>/2)<sup>1/2</sup>, reaches the yield stress, Ï<sub><i>Y</i></sub> = 1 GPa. Here <i>s</i><sub><i>ij</i></sub> = Ï<sub><i>ij</i></sub> â Ï<sub><i>kk</i></sub>Î´<sub><i>ij</i></sub>/3 is the deviatoric part of Cauchy stress, Ï<sub><i>ij</i></sub>, and ÎµÌ<sub class="stack"><i>ij</i></sub><sup class="stack">p</sup> is proportional to <i>s</i><sub><i>ij</i></sub>. Note that we assume that both chemical and plastic deformations are spin-free.</p><p>We next apply the chemo-mechanical model to simulate sodiation of crystalline black phosphorus in a rectangular shape, as shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>. Normalized by its length (along the [100] direction), its width (along the [010] direction) and height (along the [001] direction) are 0.5 and 0.35, respectively. Sodiation starts from one of the surfaces, colored in red (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figures 2">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a1âa3), by setting appropriate surface sodium concentration at relevant sodiation steps, as previously described. The back surface is set to be the symmetric plane. In the first step of sodiation, sodium fast fluxes from the {100} surface along the [100] direction (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b1). The flux patterns are markedly different from the cases when sodiation starts from the other two surfaces, {010} and {001}. As shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b2, when sodiated from the {010} surface, sodium first covers the end points of each pucker on the two {100} surfaces due to the higher diffusivity of the surface layers. Sodium atoms then enter the puckers from the two surfaces to the center by pucker-by-pucker diffusion along the [100] direction until the sodium concentration reaches 0.083 to form the Na<sub>0.25</sub>P phase. The mechanism of sodiation from the {001} surface, shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b3, is similar to that from the {010} surface, except that the sodium now diffuses layer by layer, instead of pucker by pucker. Our simulations show that intercalation induced mechanical stress is comparatively small in the first step of sodiation (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b05033/suppl_file/nl6b05033_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S1 and S2</a>). Therefore, in the next we only discuss stress generation in the second step.</p><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b05033/asset/images/medium/nl-2016-05033m_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b05033/asset/images/large/nl-2016-05033m_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. Sodiation starting from three different surfaces and the corresponding sodium flux patterns in the first step of sodiation. (a1âa3) Pristine crystalline black phosphorus blocks prior to sodiation, where the surfaces marked by red denote sodium sources. (b1âb3) Sodium fluxes in the first step of sodiation corresponding to different sodium sources in a1âa3, respectively. The material points indicate the location at which the stress evolution shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a> is extracted.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b05033/asset/images/large/nl-2016-05033m_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b05033&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p><a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figures 3">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a> and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a> plot the sodium concentration profiles at representative snapshots in the second step of sodiation and the corresponding mechanical stress, when sodiation starts from the {100} and {010} surfaces, respectively. Since sodium diffusion kinetics and stress generation are similar when sodiation starts from the {010} and {001} surfaces, we here only present one of the two cases. In <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a, when sodiation starts from the {100} surface, a diffuse interphase forms along the [100] direction, whereas a sharp interphase develops along the [010] direction when starting from the {010} surface, as shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a. In <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a, though the phosphorus near the sodium source surface is fully sodiated, the materials expand to the extent much less than that set by Î²<sub>33</sub> in the [001] direction. This deformation mode is due to the interplay between the diffusional anisotropy and chemical strain anisotropy. On the Na-rich side of the sharp interphase, the sodiated phosphorus undergoes â¼500% volume expansion. However, the partially sodiated black phosphorus (Na<sub>0.25</sub>P) in front of the sharp interphase constrains the expansion. The volume expansion along with the sharp interphase generates large mismatch stress, resulting in large plastic flow near the interphase (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b). Because of the constraint of the partially sodiated black phosphorus to the plastic flow, the materials cannot expand freely in the [001] direction, but flow to the other directions to accommodate the volume expansion. In contrast, when sodiation starts from the {100} surface, although the material still plastically yields near the diffuse interphase (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b), the constraining effect of the diffuse interphase is much weaker. As a result, the expansion in three directions conforms to the chemical strain anisotropy set by Î²<sub><i>ij</i></sub> (with a difference from the elastic deformation). The swelling phenomena manifest that these two anisotropic factors interact with one another. In particular, the anisotropic sodium diffusion generates sharp interphases along the [010] and [001] directions, which modify the intrinsic chemical strain anisotropy to shape the fully sodiated morphologies.</p><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b05033/asset/images/medium/nl-2016-05033m_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b05033/asset/images/large/nl-2016-05033m_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. (a) Sodium concentration profile and (b) the equivalent stress, (c) the hydrostatic stress, and (d) the first principal stress distributions in the second step of sodiation with sodium source on the {100} surface.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b05033/asset/images/large/nl-2016-05033m_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b05033&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b05033/asset/images/medium/nl-2016-05033m_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b05033/asset/images/large/nl-2016-05033m_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (a) Sodium concentration profile and (b) the equivalent stress, (c) the hydrostatic stress, and (d) the first principal stress distributions in the second step of sodiation with sodium source on the {010} surface.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b05033/asset/images/large/nl-2016-05033m_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b05033&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p>We next examine the evolution of the hydrostatic stress and the first principal stress during the second step of sodiation, normalized by the yield strength. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figures 3">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c plot the contours of the hydrostatic stress on the central plane perpendicular to the sodium source surfaces, corresponding to the charge mechanisms shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figures 3">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a, respectively. As a general feature for both cases, the cross sections near the interphase undergo compression (denoted by red color). The compressive region for sodiation from the {100} surface (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c) is markedly larger than that from the {010} surface (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c). This is likely due to the unloading effect behind the sharp interphase for the latter case (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b) as the sharp interphase sweeps forward. This unloading effect also leads to lower magnitude of the compression stress in the latter case (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c) than in the former one (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c). The distributions of the first principal stress for both cases are shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figures 3">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>d and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>d. Interestingly, when sodiation starts from the {100} surface, the largest first principal stress occurs in the front of the diffuse interphase in the bulk. In contrast, when sodiation starts from the {010} surface, it appears on the surface at the sharp interphase. These indicate different failure modes; interior fracture occurs when sodiation starts from the {100} surface, whereas surface fracture occurs when from the {010} surface.</p><p>To further illustrate the stress generation, we next plot the stress evolution of a representative material point, located at the center of the top surface, as shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figures 2">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a1,a2. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a,b presents the equivalent and hydrostatic stresses, along with the sodium concentration profiles, when sodiation starts from the {100} and {010} surfaces, respectively. The sodiation time is normalized by the corresponding time duration to fully sodiated the black phosphorus in the second step. As shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a, when the sodium concentration increases gradually along the [100] direction, the equivalent stress rises sharply to the yield strength. This indicates that the surface yields instantaneously at the beginning of the second step of sodiation due to the large volume expansion. The hydrostatic stress at the surface point is first in compression, then changes to tension, and finally reaches a maximum when the sodium concentration reaches â¼0.9. In <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>b, when sodiation starts from the {010} surface, the sharp interphase reaches the material point at <i>t</i> = 0.25 and sweeps through the point at <i>t</i> = 0.4. The material point yields even before the interphase arrives because of the large incompatible strain induced by the sharp interphase. It should be noted that the hydrostatic stress on the surface is compressive when the material is fully sodiated. The compressive hydrostatic stress may be caused by severe plastic flow. As mentioned previously, because the expansion is constrained in the [001] direction, newly generated volume plastically flows to the [100] and [010] directions and subsequently resulting in the overall compressive hydrostatic stress on the surface. This result demonstrates that the diffusional anisotropy causes hydrostatic stress of opposite signs on the surface when sodiation starts from different crystal facets.</p><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b05033/asset/images/medium/nl-2016-05033m_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b05033/asset/images/large/nl-2016-05033m_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. Evolution of equivalent stress (dash blue lines) and hydrostatic stress (solid blue lines) on a representative material point, along with the normalized sodium concentration (solid red lines). (a) Sodiation starts from {100} surface. (b) Sodiation starts from {010} surface.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b05033/asset/images/large/nl-2016-05033m_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b05033&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p>We further validate our model by a recent in situ TEM experiment on the sodiation of crystalline black phosphorus. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> In the experiment, the phosphorus electrode underwent potentiostatic sodiation with a natively grown Na<sub>2</sub>O layer on the sodium electrode as the solid electrolyte. The sodium electrode was then brought into contact with the black phosphorus to initiate sodiation (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig6" aria-label="Figure 6">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>a1). Consistent with the experimental settings, we set a small region on the {100} surface as the sodium source boundary condition in our model. The sodium concentration contour at a representative sodiation time in the first step of sodiation is plotted in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig6" aria-label="Figure 6">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>b1. The first principal stresses in the first step are much smaller than those in the second step (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig6" aria-label="Figures 6">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>c1,d1). At the initial stage of the second step, as shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig6" aria-label="Figure 6">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>b2, a diffuse interphase forms along the [100] direction, while a sharp interphase along [010] direction. This sodiation mode can thus be regarded as a combination of the cases with sodium sources on both the {100} and {010} surfaces. Because sodiation proceeds much faster in the [100] direction than in the [010] direction, a sharp interphase parallel to the [100] direction is developed with nearly fully sodiated phase behind the interphase (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig6" aria-label="Figure 6">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>b3), which compares with the experiment observation shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig6" aria-label="Figure 6">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>a3. As indicated by the dash lines in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig6" aria-label="Figure 6">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>b2âb4, we depict the first principal stress profiles on the cross sections containing diffuse and sharp interphases in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig6" aria-label="Figure 6">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>c2âc4, d2âd4, respectively. The stress profiles share the similar general features as observed in the single-sodium-source cases, that is, high first principal stresses occur in the front of the diffuse interphase in the bulk and near the sharp interphase on the surface. However, the distribution of the first principal stress in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig6" aria-label="Figure 6">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>c3 is different from that of the single-directional case (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>d) due to a cancellation effect. Specifically, because compression occurs inside the diffuse interphase (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>d) and tension behind the sharp interphase (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>d) in the single-surface-source sodiation cases, as discussed previously, these two stresses cancel out to certain extent, resulting in a relatively low stress state far from the sodium source behind the sharp interphase (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig6" aria-label="Figure 6">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>c3, pointed by the arrow). The largest first principal stress in this two-surface-source sodiation case appears at the fully sodiation stage in the bulk (c4 and d4, pointed by the arrows), indicating interior fracture. However, the nucleation site of fracture, indicated by the location with the highest tension, is different from the case when sodiation starts the {100} surface (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>d). Our model further predicts that the expansions are 1.36%, 91.7%, and 153.6% along the [100], [010], and [001] directions, respectively, which are in good agreement with the experimentally measured data 0%, 92%, and 160%, respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a></p><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b05033/asset/images/medium/nl-2016-05033m_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b05033/asset/images/large/nl-2016-05033m_0006.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. Comparisons between experiment and simulation results during the sodiation of black phosphorus. (a1âa4) TEM images showing the volumetric expansion of black phosphorus during sodiation. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> (b1âb4) The sodium concentration profiles in the first step (b1) and in the second step (b2âb4) of sodiation, respectively. (c1âc4) The first principal stresses on the {010} cross-section the first (c1) and second step (c2âc4) of sodiation. (d1âd4) The first principal stresses on the {100} cross-section in the first (d1) and second (d2âd4) step of sodiation. The cross sections are indicated in (b1âb4) by the black dash lines. Regions of low stress due to the cancellation effect (c3) and of the largest first principal stresses at the fully sodiation stage (c4 and d4) are marked by dash boxes and pointed by arrows.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b05033/asset/images/large/nl-2016-05033m_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b05033&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="last">We conclude that the apparent anisotropic swelling of sodiated black phosphorus is originated from the coupled effect of the two intrinsic anisotropies, that is, sodium diffusional directionality and chemical strain anisotropy. In particular, the sodium diffusional directionality in the layered structure generates sharp interphases along the [010] and [001] directions, which modify the chemical strain anisotropy to shape the fully sodiated morphology. Though not discussed here, the chemical strain anisotropy would reciprocally modulate stress generation, which in turn modifies the diffusional anisotropy, causing sodiation speedup or retardation, as seen in lithiation of Si and Ge. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref53 ref54 ref55 ref56'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref53 ref54 ref55 ref56" aria-label="reference 53 to 56">(53-56)</a> The coupling effect renders substantial difference in the stress generation and degradation when sodiation starts from different crystal facets: interior fracture when sodiation starts from a {100} facet but surface fracture when from a {010} or {001} facet. When sodiation starts simultaneously from two different facets, the interaction between the diffuse and sharp interphases alter the stress landscape, and consequently modify the fracture mechanism and/or nucleation sites of fracture. The modeling framework developed here is widely applicable to the study of lithiation or sodiation of layered crystals. Our findings provide rational guidance to mitigate the degradation of black phosphorus as the promising anode for the next-generation NIBs with improved durability and cyclability.</p><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i1">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge on the <a href="/" class="ext-link">ACS Publications website</a> at DOI: <a href="/doi/abs/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b05033" class="ext-link">10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b05033</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Additional material constants and stress distributions in the first step of sodiation (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b05033/suppl_file/nl6b05033_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b05033/suppl_file/nl6b05033_si_001.pdf">nl6b05033_si_001.pdf (306.23 kb)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b05033" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

                        cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrode materials for rechargeable sodium-ion batteries: potential alternatives to current lithium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kim, Sung-Wook; Seo, Dong-Hwa; Ma, Xiaohua; Ceder, Gerbrand; Kang, Kisuk</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Energy Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2012</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">2</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">7</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">710-721</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-Blackwell</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Lithium (Li)-ion batteries (LIB) have governed the current worldwide rechargeable battery market due to their outstanding energy and power capability.  In particular, the LIB's role in enabling elec. vehicles (EVs) has been highlighted to replace the current oil-driven vehicles in order to reduce the usage of oil resources and generation of CO2 gases.  Unlike Li, sodium is one of the more abundant elements on Earth and exhibits similar chem. properties to Li, indicating that Na chem. could be applied to a similar battery system.  In the 1970s-80s, both Na-ion and Li-ion electrodes were investigated, but the higher energy d. of Li-ion cells made them more applicable to small, portable electronic devices, and research efforts for rechargeable batteries have been mainly concd. on LIB since then.  Recently, research interest in Na-ion batteries (NIB) has been resurrected, driven by new applications with requirements different from those in portable electronics, and to address the concern on Li abundance.  In this article, both neg. and pos. electrode materials in NIB are briefly reviewed.  While the voltage is generally lower and the vol. change upon Na removal or insertion is larger for Na-intercalation electrodes, compared to their Li equiv., the power capability can vary depending on the crystal structures.  It is concluded that cost-effective NIB can partially replace LIB, but requires further investigation and improvement.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGonnrfiSUpaRrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0licmaZsM5m79A"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XhtVOrtLjF&amp;md5=485b20393bb8876b2f69ca708aeba606</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref2'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1002/adfm.201200691"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Slater, M.
