                        cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A 3.8-V earth-abundant sodium battery electrode</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Barpanda Prabeer; Oyama Gosuke; Nishimura Shin-ichi; Yamada Atsuo; Chung Sai-Cheong</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature communications</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2014</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">5</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">4358</span>
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn"></span>.
    </div><div class="casAbstract">Rechargeable lithium batteries have ushered the wireless revolution over last two decades and are now matured to enable green automobiles.  However, the growing concern on scarcity and large-scale applications of lithium resources have steered effort to realize sustainable sodium-ion batteries, Na and Fe being abundant and low-cost charge carrier and redox centre, respectively.  However, their performance is limited owing to low operating voltage and sluggish kinetics.  Here we report a hitherto-unknown material with entirely new composition and structure with the first alluaudite-type sulphate framework, Na2Fe2(SO4)3, registering the highest-ever Fe(3+)/Fe(2+) redox potential at 3.8 V (versus Na, and hence 4.1 V versus Li) along with fast rate kinetics.  Rare-metal-free Na-ion rechargeable battery system compatible with the present Li-ion battery is now in realistic scope without sacrificing high energy density and high power, and paves way for discovery of new earth-abundant sustainable cathodes for large-scale batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=LOZGTtr2ZcRyPsIeUGwJuwvupmecNw_lfW6udTcc2ebe5HOMoyPtlbntkvexoksv"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A280%3ADC%252BC2cbjtFWgtg%253D%253D&amp;md5=bed08f99d54ab89b110163e471047494</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref5'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit5" data-doi="10.1002/adfm.201100854"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Komaba, S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Murata, W.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ishikawa, T.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yabuuchi, N.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ozeki, T.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Nakayama, T.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ogata, A.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Gotoh, K.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Fujiwara, K.</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Adv.                         cas_cit12R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">12</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Predicting capacity of hard carbon anodes in sodium-ion batteries using porosity measurements</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Bommier, Clement; Luo, Wei; Gao, Wen-Yang; Greaney, Alex; Ma, Shengqian; Ji, Xiulei</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Carbon</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2014</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">76</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">165-174</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CRBNAH</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0008-6223</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">We report an inverse relationship between measurable porosity values and reversible capacity from sucrose-derived hard carbon as an anode for sodium-ion batteries.  Materials with low measureable pore vols. and surface areas obtained by N2 sorption yield higher reversible capacities.  Conversely, increasing measurable porosity and sp. surface area leads to sharp decreases in reversible capacity.  Utilizing a low porosity material, we thus are able to obtain a reversible capacity of 335 mA-h/g.  These findings suggest that sodium-ion storage is highly dependent on the absence of pores detectable by N2 sorption in sucrose-derived carbon.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpP32REpm8pmbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgIg5CAG6XESA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXnslOls7w%253D&amp;md5=7a7d2948f730529be5550c74672e9bf2</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref13"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref13'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">13</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit13" data-doi="10.1002/anie.201209689"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Qian, J.                         cas_cit14R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">14</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Stable cycling of double-walled silicon nanotube battery anodes through solid-electrolyte interphase control</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wu, Hui; Chan, Gerentt; Choi, Jang Wook; Ryu, Ill; Yao, Yan; McDowell, Matthew T.; Lee, Seok Woo; Jackson, Ariel; Yang, Yuan; Hu, Liangbing; Cui, Yi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Nanotechnology</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2012</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">7</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">5</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">310-315</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NNAABX</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1748-3387</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Publishing Group</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Although the performance of lithium ion-batteries continues to improve, their energy d. and cycle life remain insufficient for applications in consumer electronics, transport, and large-scale renewable energy storage.  Silicon has a large charge storage capacity and this makes it an attractive anode material, but pulverization during cycling and an unstable solid-electrolyte interphase has limited the cycle life of silicon anodes to hundreds of cycles.  Here, it is shown that anodes consisting of an active silicon nanotube surrounded by an ion-permeable silicon oxide shell can cycle over 6,000 times in half cells while retaining &gt; 85% of their initial capacity.  The outer surface of the silicon nanotube is prevented from expansion by the oxide shell, and the expanding inner surface is not exposed to the electrolyte, resulting in a stable solid-electrolyte interphase.  Batteries contg. these double-walled silicon nanotube anodes exhibit charge capacities approx. eight times larger than conventional carbon anodes and charging rates of up to 20C (a rate of 1C corresponds to complete charge or discharge in one hour).</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqaEH2gqjH0ZbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgIg5CAG6XESA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XksVentLc%253D&amp;md5=13d632e4d2279068afe4a35d7fe7d4c0</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref15"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">15</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit15" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201300496"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yi, R.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Dai, F.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Gordin, M.                 <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ADEMBC</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1614-6840</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-Blackwell</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Silicon has been intensively pursued as the most promising anode material for Li-ion batteries due to its high theor. capacity of 3579 mAh/g.  Micro-sized Si-C composites composed of nanoscale primary building blocks are attractive Si-based anodes for practical application because they not only achieve excellent cycling stability, but also offer both gravimetric and volumetric capacity.  However, the effects of key parameters in designing such materials on their electrochem. performance are unknown and how to optimize them thus remains to be explored.  Herein, the influence of Si nanoscale building block size and carbon coating on the electrochem. performance of the micro-sized Si-C composites is investigated.  It is found that the crit. Si building block size is 15 nm, which enables a high capacity without compromising the cycling stability, and that carbon coating at higher temp. improves the first cycle coulombic efficiency (CE) and the rate capability.  Corresponding reasons underlying electrochem. performance are revealed by various characterizations.  Combining both optimized Si building block size and carbon coating temp., the resultant composite can sustain 600 cycles at 1.2 A/g with a fixed lithiation capacity of 1200 mAh/g, the best cycling performance with such a high capacity for micro-sized Si-based anodes.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrNErowEpuKJ7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgQvyIt_sip6A"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXhslGmtLbP&amp;md5=64323ca69920f7dad8384d1892fef9c5</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref16"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">16</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit16" data-doi="10.1016/j.eml.2016.03.006"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Jia, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, T.</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Extreme Mech.                         cas_cit18R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">18</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Size-dependent fracture of Si nanowire battery anodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ryu, Ill; Choi, Jang Wook; Cui, Yi; Nix, William D.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the Mechanics and Physics of Solids</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">We use a unique transmission electron microscope (TEM) technique to show that Si nanowires (NWs) with diams. in the range of a few hundred nanometers can be fully lithiated and delithiated without fracture, in spite of the large vol. changes that occur in this process.  By analyzing the stresses assocd. with lithiation and delithiation we conclude that the process does not occur by the growth of discrete cryst. phases; rather it occurs by amorphization of the Si NWs followed by diffusion of Li into the structure.  By accounting for the large deformation assocd. with this process and by including the effects of pressure gradients on the diffusion of Li, we show that Si NWs with diams. less than about 300 nm could not fracture even if pre-existing cracks were present in the NW.  These predictions appear to be in good agreement with the expt.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqX83Hq6E89cbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgQvyIt_sip6A"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3MXhtVCrt7jL&amp;md5=c240f3cbec54c17a1bce6e76309c7c52</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref19"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref19'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">19</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit19" data-doi="10.1073/pnas.1201088109"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lee, S.                         cas_cit22R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">22</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">In situ observation of the electrochemical lithiation of a single SnO2 nanowire electrode</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Huang, Jian Yu; Zhong, Li; Wang, Chong Min; Sullivan, John P.; Xu, Wu; Zhang, Li Qiang; Mao, Scott X.; Hudak, Nicholas S.; Liu, Xiao Hua; Subramanian, Arunkumar; Fan, Hongyou; Qi, Liang; Kushima, Akihiro; Li, Ju</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Science (Washington, DC, United States)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2010</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">330</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">6010</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1515-1520</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">SCIEAS</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0036-8075</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Association for the Advancement of Science</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">We report the creation of a nanoscale electrochem. device inside a transmission electron microscope-consisting of a single tin dioxide (SnO2) nanowire anode, an ionic liq. electrolyte, and a bulk lithium cobalt dioxide (LiCoO2) cathode-and the in situ observation of the lithiation of the SnO2 nanowire during electrochem. charging.  Upon charging, a reaction front propagated progressively along the nanowire, causing the nanowire to swell, elongate, and spiral.  The reaction front is a "Medusa zone" contg. a high d. of mobile dislocations, which are continuously nucleated and absorbed at the moving front.  This dislocation cloud indicates large in-plane misfit stresses and is a structural precursor to electrochem. driven solid-state amorphization.  Because lithiation-induced vol. expansion, plasticity, and pulverization of electrode materials are the major mech. effects that plague the performance and lifetime of high-capacity anodes in lithium-ion batteries, our observations provide important mechanistic insight for the design of advanced batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGr_6CVVGvaJ-bVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgQvyIt_sip6A"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3cXhsFaht7zM&amp;md5=5133171b13221aa2bd933f452e34f203</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref23"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref23'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">23</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit23" data-doi="10.1016/j.carbon.2012.04.025"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liu, X.                 10.1016/j.carbon.2012.04.025</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar%3Fhl%3Den%26q%3DLiu%252C%2BX.%2BH.%253B%2BWang%252C%2BJ.%2BW.%253B%2BLiu%252C%2BY.%253B%2BZheng%252C%2BH.%253B%2BKushima%252C%2BA.%253B%2BHuang%252C%2BS.%253B%2BZhu%252C%2BT.%253B%2BMao%252C%2BS.%2BX.%253B%2BLi%252C%2BJ.%253B%2BZhang%252C%2BS.%253B%2BLu%252C%2BW.%253B%2BTour%252C%2BJ.%2BM.%253B%2BHuang%252C%2BJ.%2BY.Carbon%2B2012%252C%2B50%252C%2B3836%25E2%2580%25933844&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facs.nanolett.6b05033&amp;doiOfLink=10.1016%2Fj.carbon.2012.04.025&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit23R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">23</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">In situ transmission electron microscopy of electrochemical lithiation, delithiation and deformation of individual graphene nanoribbons</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Liu, Xiao Hua; Wang, Jiang Wei; Liu, Yang; Zheng, He; Kushima, Akihiro; Huang, Shan; Zhu, Ting; Mao, Scott X.; Li, Ju; Zhang, Sulin; Lu, Wei; Tour, James M.; Huang, Jian Yu</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Carbon</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2012</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">50</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">10</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3836-3844</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CRBNAH</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0008-6223</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">An in situ transmission electron microscopy study is reported of the electrochem. behavior of few-layer graphene nanoribbons (GNRs) synthesized by longitudinal splitting the multi-walled carbon nanotubes (MWCNTs).  Upon lithiation, the GNRs were covered by a nanocryst. lithium oxide layer attached to the surfaces and edges of the GNRs, most of which were removed upon delithiation, indicating that the lithiation/delithiation processes occurred predominantly at the surfaces of GNRs.  The lithiated GNRs were mech. robust during the tension and compression tests, in sharp contrast to the easy and brittle fracture of the lithiated MWCNTs.  This difference is attributed to the unconfined stacking of planar carbon layers in GNRs leading to a weak coupling between the intralayer and interlayer deformations, as opposed to the cylindrically confined carbon nanotubes where the interlayer lithium produces large tensile hoop stresses within the circumferentially-closed carbon layers, causing the ease of brittle fracture.  These results suggest substantial promise of graphene for building durable batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrhIoXomNzvvrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgQvyIt_sip6A"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38Xmt12iur4%253D&amp;md5=75bec3f7b0901f47b840be8a9a784951</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref24"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref24'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">24</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit24" data-doi="10.1021/nl402644w"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liang, W.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hong, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yang, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Fan, F.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liu, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Huang, J.                         cas_cit30R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">30</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Anisotropic Swelling and Fracture of Silicon Nanowires during Lithiation</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Liu, Xiao Hua; Zheng, He; Zhong, Li; Huang, Shan; Karki, Khim; Zhang, Li Qiang; Liu, Yang; Kushima, Akihiro; Liang, Wen Tao; Wang, Jiang Wei; Cho, Jeong-Hyun; Epstein, Eric; Dayeh, Shadi A.; Picraux, S. Tom; Zhu, Ting; Li, Ju; Sullivan, John P.; Cumings, John; Wang, Chunsheng; Mao, Scott X.; Ye, Zhi Zhen; Zhang, Sulin; Huang, Jian Yu</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Letters</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2011</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">11</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The authors report direct observation of an unexpected anisotropic swelling of Si nanowires during lithiation against either a solid electrolyte with a Li counter-electrode or a liq. electrolyte with a LiCoO2 counter-electrode.  Such anisotropic expansion is attributed to the interfacial processes of accommodating large volumetric strains at the lithiation reaction front that depend sensitively on the crystallog. orientation.  This anisotropic swelling results in lithiated Si nanowires with a remarkable dumbbell-shaped cross section, which develops due to plastic flow and an ensuing necking instability that is induced by the tensile hoop stress buildup in the lithiated shell.  The plasticity-driven morphol. instabilities often lead to fracture in lithiated nanowires, now captured in video.  These results provide important insight into the battery degrdn. mechanisms.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGohhMVk4OC8MbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhU63FgMp_N9w"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3MXotlGnsbo%253D&amp;md5=4c801363ba407d8179cba96d569b0dc5</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref31"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref31'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">31</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit31" data-doi="10.1021/acsnano.5b01681"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Luo, L.                         cas_cit33R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">33</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Inward lithium-ion breathing of hierarchically porous silicon anodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Xiao, Qiangfeng; Gu, Meng; Yang, Hui; Li, Bing; Zhang, Cunman; Liu, Yang; Liu, Fang; Dai, Fang; Yang, Li; Liu, Zhongyi; Xiao, Xingcheng; Liu, Gao; Zhao, Peng; Zhang, Sulin; Wang, Chongmin; Lu, Yunfeng; Cai, Mei</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Communications</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">6</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">8844</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NCAOBW</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2041-1723</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Publishing Group</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Silicon has been identified as a highly promising anode for next-generation lithium-ion batteries (LIBs).  The key challenge for Si anodes is large vol. change during the lithiation/delithiation cycle that results in chemomech. degrdn. and subsequent rapid capacity fading.  Here we report a novel fabrication method for hierarchically porous Si nanospheres (hp-SiNSs), which consist of a porous shell and a hollow core.  On charge/discharge cycling, the hp-SiNSs accommodate the vol. change through reversible inward Li breathing with negligible particle-level outward expansion.  Our mechanics anal. revealed that such inward expansion is enabled by the much stiffer lithiated layer than the unlithiated porous layer.  LIBs assembled with the hp-SiNSs exhibit high capacity, high power and long cycle life, which is superior to the current com. Si-based anode materials.  The low-cost synthesis approach provides a new avenue for the rational design of hierarchically porous structures with unique materials properties.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGo7mNP5yCcxv7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhU63FgMp_N9w"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhslyht7fN&amp;md5=ec70cd6bfde00198bcc3e6ff9f9c6d2a</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref34"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref34'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">34</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit34" data-doi="10.1038/nnano.2007.411"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Chan, C.                         cas_cit34R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">34</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">High-performance lithium battery anodes using silicon nanowires</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Chan, Candace K.; Peng, Hailin; Liu, Gao; McIlwrath, Kevin; Zhang, Xiao Feng; Huggins, Robert A.; Cui, Yi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Nanotechnology</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2008</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">3</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">31-35</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NNAABX</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1748-3387</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Publishing Group</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The rechargeable Li batteries with higher energy capacity and longer cycle life are necessary for application in portable electronic devices, elec. vehicles and implantable medical devices.  Si is an attractive anode material for Li batteries because it has a low discharge potential and the highest known theor. charge capacity - 4,200 mA-h/g.  Although this is &gt;10 times higher than existing graphite anodes and much larger than various nitride and oxide materials, Si anodes have limited applications because Si's vol. changes by 400% upon insertion and extn. of Li which results in pulverization and capacity fading.  Si nanowire electrodes circumvent these issues as they can accommodate large strain without pulverization, provide good electronic contact and conduction and have short Li insertion distances.  The authors achieved the theor. charge capacity for Si anodes and maintained a discharge capacity close to 75% of this max. with little fading during cycling.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqOQQPx-rSMh7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhU63FgMp_N9w"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD1cXmvVGh&amp;md5=45ddde65c917525f2915f348c45e1e56</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref35"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref35'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">35</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit35" data-doi="10.1021/nl3014814"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liu, N.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wu, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">McDowell, M.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Efficient mech. energy harvesters enable various wearable devices and auxiliary energy supply.                         cas_cit38R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">38</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A phosphorene-graphene hybrid material as a high-capacity anode for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Sun, Jie; Lee, Hyun-Wook; Pasta, Mauro; Yuan, Hongtao; Zheng, Guangyuan; Sun, Yongming; Li, Yuzhang; Cui, Yi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Nanotechnology</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-ion batteries have recently attracted significant attention as an alternative to Li-ion batteries because Na sources do not present the geopolitical issues that Li sources might.  Although recent reports on cathode materials for Na-ion batteries demonstrated performances comparable to their Li-ion counterparts, the major scientific challenge for a competitive Na-ion battery technol. is to develop viable anode materials.  A hybrid material made out of a few phosphorene layers sandwiched between graphene layers shows a specific capacity of 2 440 mA h g-1 (calcd. using the mass of P only) at a c.d. of 0.05 A g-1 and an 83% capacity retention after 100 cycles while operating between 0 and 1.5 V.                         cas_cit42R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">42</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A chemo-mechanical model of lithiation in silicon</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yang, Hui; Fan, Feifei; Liang, Wentao; Guo, Xu; Zhu, Ting; Zhang, Sulin</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the Mechanics and Physics of Solids</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2014</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">70</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">349-361</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JMPSA8</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0022-5096</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">We present a chemo-mech. model to investigate the lithiation-induced phase transformation, morphol. evolution, stress generation and fracture in cryst. silicon nanowires (c-SiNWs).  The model couples lithium (Li) diffusion with elasto-plastic deformation in a three-dimensional (3D) setting.  Several key features obsd. from recent transmission electron microscopy (TEM) studies are incorporated into the model, including the sharp interface between the lithiated amorphous shell and unlithiated cryst. core, crystallog. orientation dependent Li-Si reaction rate, and large-strain plasticity.  Our simulation results demonstrate that the model faithfully predicts the anisotropic swelling of lithiated SiNWs obsd. from previous exptl. studies.  Stress anal. from the finite-deformation model reveals that the SiNWs are prone to surface fracture at the angular sites where two adjacent {110} facets intersect, consistent with previous exptl. observations.  The mechanistic understanding of the morphol. evolution and stress generation sheds light on the design of failure-resistant nanostructured electrodes.  Our model offers a framework for the study of the chemo-mech. degrdn. in high-capacity electrode materials.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpV-GPEw3VAObVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljRTZWUJJAu3g"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXhtlWrsrbN&amp;md5=71bf1f9d4aeafd2bd6cfaba577e628ed</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref43"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref43'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">43</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit43" data-doi="10.1021/nn202071y"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liu, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zheng, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liu, X.                         cas_cit48R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">48</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Two-Phase Electrochemical Lithiation in Amorphous Silicon</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Jiang Wei; He, Yu; Fan, Feifei; Liu, Xiao Hua; Xia, Shuman; Liu, Yang; Harris, C. Thomas; Li, Hong; Huang, Jian Yu; Mao, Scott X.; Zhu, Ting</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Letters</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">13</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">2</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">709-715</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NALEFD</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1530-6984</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Lithium-ion batteries have revolutionized portable electronics and will be a key to electrifying transport vehicles and delivering renewable electricity.  Amorphous silicon (a-Si) is being intensively studied as a high-capacity anode material for next-generation lithium-ion batteries.  Its lithiation has been widely thought to occur through a single-phase mechanism with gentle Li profiles, thus offering a significant potential for mitigating pulverization and capacity fade.  Here, a surprising two-phase process is discovered of electrochem. lithiation in a-Si by using in situ transmission electron microscopy.  The lithiation occurs by the movement of a sharp phase boundary between the a-Si reactant and an amorphous LixSi (a-LixSi, x â¼ 2.5) product.  Such a striking amorphous-amorphous interface exists until the remaining a-Si is consumed.  Then a second step of lithiation sets in without a visible interface, resulting in the final product of a-LixSi (x â¼ 3.75).  It is shown that the two-phase lithiation can be the fundamental mechanism underpinning the anomalous morphol. change of microfabricated a-Si electrodes, i.e., from a disk shape to a dome shape.  The results represent a significant step toward the understanding of the electrochem. driven reaction and degrdn. in amorphous materials, which is crit. to the development of microstructurally stable electrodes for high-performance lithium-ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGq03Vw14dqs0LVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljRTZWUJJAu3g"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXoslOhtA%253D%253D&amp;md5=3438f8bafdffc05e0c09838a1cd7a88b</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref49"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref49'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">49</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit49" data-doi="10.1021/nl3044508"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">McDowell, M.                 <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JMPSA8</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0022-5096</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Recent expts. and first-principles calcns. show the two-step lithiation of amorphous silicon (a-Si).  In the first step, the lithiation progresses by the movement of a sharp phase boundary between a pristine a-Si phase and an intermediate LiÎ·Si phase until the a-Si phase is fully consumed.  Then the second step sets in without a visible interface, with the LiÎ·Si phase continuously lithiating to a Li3.75Si phase.  This unique feature of lithiation is believed to have important consequences for mech. durability of a-Si anodes in lithium ion batteries, however the mechanistic understanding of such consequences is still elusive so far.  Here, we reveal an intrinsic stress mitigation mechanism due to elastic softening during two-step lithiation of a-Si, via chemo-mech. modeling.  We find that lithiation-induced elastic softening of a-Si leads to effective stress mitigation in the second step of lithiation.  These mechanistic findings allow for the first time to quant. predict the crit. size of an a-Si anode below which the anode becomes immune to lithiation-induced fracture, which is in good agreement with exptl. observations.  Further studies on lithiation kinetics suggest that the two-step lithiation also results in a lower stress-induced energy barrier for lithiation.  The present study reveals the phys. underpinnings of previously unexplained favorable lithiation kinetics and fracture behavior of a-Si anodes, and thus sheds light on quant. design guidelines toward high-performance anodes for lithium ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpzXrWKTar13rVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhYXzxsvLtOCA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28Xlslent70%253D&amp;md5=9441c1f753973fce53e9bd732d6c5ae7</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref51"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref51'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">51</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit51" data-doi="10.1016/j.actamat.2013.04.007"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Huang, S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Fan, F.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhang, S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhu, T.</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Acta Mater.</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2013</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">61</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">4354</span><span class="NLM_x">â</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">4364</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/j.actamat.2013.04.007</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar%3Fhl%3Den%26q%3DHuang%252C%2BS.%253B%2BFan%252C%2BF.%253B%2BLi%252C%2BJ.%253B%2BZhang%252C%2BS.%253B%2BZhu%252C%2BT.Acta%2BMater.%2B2013%252C%2B61%252C%2B4354%25E2%2580%25934364&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facs.nanolett.6b05033&amp;doiOfLink=10.1016%2Fj.actamat.2013.04.007&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit51R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">51</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Stress generation during lithiation of high-capacity electrode particles in lithium ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Huang, S.; Fan, F.; Li, J.; Zhang, S.; Zhu, T.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Acta Materialia</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">61</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">12</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">4354-4364</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ACMAFD</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1359-6454</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A model is developed to study the stress generation in a spherical particle subjected to lithium insertion.  The model accounts for both the plastic deformation and the coexistence of lithium-poor and lithium-rich phases with a sharp and curved phase boundary.  Such two-phase and inelastic deformation characteristics often arise during lithiation of cryst. particles with high capacity.  A flexible sigmoid function is used to create the lithium profile with a step-like change in lithium concn., mimicking a sharp phase boundary that separates a pristine core and a lithiated shell in the particle.  The mechanics results, obtained by an analytic formulation and finite difference calcns., show the development of tensile hoop stress in the surface layer of the lithiated shell.  This hoop tension provides the driving force of surface cracking, as obsd. by in situ TEM.  The two-phase lithiation model is further compared with the single-phase one, which assumes a gradual and smooth variation in radial lithium distributions, and thus predicts only hoop compression in the surface layer of the particle.  Furthermore, the effect of dilatational vs. unidirectional lithiation strains in the two-phase model is studied, thereby underscoring the crit. role of anisotropy of lithiation strain in controlling stress generation in high-capacity electrodes for lithium ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGquIuGyAuN0ZrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhYXzxsvLtOCA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXntVaqtrY%253D&amp;md5=caf6f8f13e51d15c89c5707737b34a5f</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref52"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref52'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">52</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit52" data-doi="10.1103/PhysRevB.86.035105"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Appalakondaiah, S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Vaitheeswaran, G.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lebegue, S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Christensen, N.                         rightTab-cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrode materials for rechargeable sodium-ion batteries: potential alternatives to current lithium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kim, Sung-Wook; Seo, Dong-Hwa; Ma, Xiaohua; Ceder, Gerbrand; Kang, Kisuk</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Energy Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2012</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">2</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">7</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">710-721</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ADEMBC</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-Blackwell</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Lithium (Li)-ion batteries (LIB) have governed the current worldwide rechargeable battery market due to their outstanding energy and power capability.  In particular, the LIB's role in enabling elec. vehicles (EVs) has been highlighted to replace the current oil-driven vehicles in order to reduce the usage of oil resources and generation of CO2 gases.  Unlike Li, sodium is one of the more abundant elements on Earth and exhibits similar chem. properties to Li, indicating that Na chem. could be applied to a similar battery system.  In the 1970s-80s, both Na-ion and Li-ion electrodes were investigated, but the higher energy d. of Li-ion cells made them more applicable to small, portable electronic devices, and research efforts for rechargeable batteries have been mainly concd. on LIB since then.  Recently, research interest in Na-ion batteries (NIB) has been resurrected, driven by new applications with requirements different from those in portable electronics, and to address the concern on Li abundance.  In this article, both neg. and pos. electrode materials in NIB are briefly reviewed.  While the voltage is generally lower and the vol. change upon Na removal or insertion is larger for Na-intercalation electrodes, compared to their Li equiv., the power capability can vary depending on the crystal structures.  It is concluded that cost-effective NIB can partially replace LIB, but requires further investigation and improvement.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGonnrfiSUpaRrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljgt1bFkEuMEQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XhtVOrtLjF&amp;md5=485b20393bb8876b2f69ca708aeba606</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref2" class="rightTabRefNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit2" data-doi="10.1002/adfm.201200691"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Slater, M.                         rightTab-cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A 3.8-V earth-abundant sodium battery electrode</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Barpanda Prabeer; Oyama Gosuke; Nishimura Shin-ichi; Yamada Atsuo; Chung Sai-Cheong</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature communications</span>

    </div><div class="casAbstract">Rechargeable lithium batteries have ushered the wireless revolution over last two decades and are now matured to enable green automobiles.  However, the growing concern on scarcity and large-scale applications of lithium resources have steered effort to realize sustainable sodium-ion batteries, Na and Fe being abundant and low-cost charge carrier and redox centre, respectively.  However, their performance is limited owing to low operating voltage and sluggish kinetics.  Here we report a hitherto-unknown material with entirely new composition and structure with the first alluaudite-type sulphate framework, Na2Fe2(SO4)3, registering the highest-ever Fe(3+)/Fe(2+) redox potential at 3.8 V (versus Na, and hence 4.1 V versus Li) along with fast rate kinetics.  Rare-metal-free Na-ion rechargeable battery system compatible with the present Li-ion battery is now in realistic scope without sacrificing high energy density and high power, and paves way for discovery of new earth-abundant sustainable cathodes for large-scale batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=LOZGTtr2ZcRyPsIeUGwJuwvupmecNw_lfW6udTcc2easnt7F7jK-ibntkvexoksv"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A280%3ADC%252BC2cbjtFWgtg%253D%253D&amp;md5=bed08f99d54ab89b110163e471047494</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref5" class="rightTabRefNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit5" data-doi="10.1002/adfm.201100854"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Komaba, S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Murata, W.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ishikawa, T.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yabuuchi, N.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ozeki, T.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Nakayama, T.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ogata, A.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Gotoh, K.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Fujiwara, K.</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Adv.                 10.1016/j.carbon.2014.04.064</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit12R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">12</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Predicting capacity of hard carbon anodes in sodium-ion batteries using porosity measurements</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Bommier, Clement; Luo, Wei; Gao, Wen-Yang; Greaney, Alex; Ma, Shengqian; Ji, Xiulei</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Carbon</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2014</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">76</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">165-174</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CRBNAH</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0008-6223</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">We report an inverse relationship between measurable porosity values and reversible capacity from sucrose-derived hard carbon as an anode for sodium-ion batteries.  Materials with low measureable pore vols. and surface areas obtained by N2 sorption yield higher reversible capacities.  Conversely, increasing measurable porosity and sp. surface area leads to sharp decreases in reversible capacity.  Utilizing a low porosity material, we thus are able to obtain a reversible capacity of 335 mA-h/g.  These findings suggest that sodium-ion storage is highly dependent on the absence of pores detectable by N2 sorption in sucrose-derived carbon.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpP32REpm8pmbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljhTS9uIvcbfA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXnslOls7w%253D&amp;md5=7a7d2948f730529be5550c74672e9bf2</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref13"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref13" class="rightTabRefNumLink">13</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit13" data-doi="10.1002/anie.201209689"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Qian, J. F.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wu, X. Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Cao, Y.                         rightTab-cas_cit14R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">14</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Stable cycling of double-walled silicon nanotube battery anodes through solid-electrolyte interphase control</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wu, Hui; Chan, Gerentt; Choi, Jang Wook; Ryu, Ill; Yao, Yan; McDowell, Matthew T.; Lee, Seok Woo; Jackson, Ariel; Yang, Yuan; Hu, Liangbing; Cui, Yi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Nanotechnology</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Publishing Group</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Although the performance of lithium ion-batteries continues to improve, their energy d. and cycle life remain insufficient for applications in consumer electronics, transport, and large-scale renewable energy storage.  Silicon has a large charge storage capacity and this makes it an attractive anode material, but pulverization during cycling and an unstable solid-electrolyte interphase has limited the cycle life of silicon anodes to hundreds of cycles.  Here, it is shown that anodes consisting of an active silicon nanotube surrounded by an ion-permeable silicon oxide shell can cycle over 6,000 times in half cells while retaining &gt; 85% of their initial capacity.  The outer surface of the silicon nanotube is prevented from expansion by the oxide shell, and the expanding inner surface is not exposed to the electrolyte, resulting in a stable solid-electrolyte interphase.  Batteries contg. these double-walled silicon nanotube anodes exhibit charge capacities approx. eight times larger than conventional carbon anodes and charging rates of up to 20C (a rate of 1C corresponds to complete charge or discharge in one hour).</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqaEH2gqjH0ZbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljhTS9uIvcbfA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XksVentLc%253D&amp;md5=13d632e4d2279068afe4a35d7fe7d4c0</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref15"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref15" class="rightTabRefNumLink">15</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit15" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201300496"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yi, R.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Dai, F.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Gordin, M.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Silicon has been intensively pursued as the most promising anode material for Li-ion batteries due to its high theor. capacity of 3579 mAh/g.  Micro-sized Si-C composites composed of nanoscale primary building blocks are attractive Si-based anodes for practical application because they not only achieve excellent cycling stability, but also offer both gravimetric and volumetric capacity.  However, the effects of key parameters in designing such materials on their electrochem. performance are unknown and how to optimize them thus remains to be explored.  Herein, the influence of Si nanoscale building block size and carbon coating on the electrochem. performance of the micro-sized Si-C composites is investigated.  It is found that the crit. Si building block size is 15 nm, which enables a high capacity without compromising the cycling stability, and that carbon coating at higher temp. improves the first cycle coulombic efficiency (CE) and the rate capability.  Corresponding reasons underlying electrochem. performance are revealed by various characterizations.  Combining both optimized Si building block size and carbon coating temp., the resultant composite can sustain 600 cycles at 1.2 A/g with a fixed lithiation capacity of 1200 mAh/g, the best cycling performance with such a high capacity for micro-sized Si-based anodes.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrNErowEpuKJ7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljhTS9uIvcbfA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXhslGmtLbP&amp;md5=64323ca69920f7dad8384d1892fef9c5</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref16"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref16" class="rightTabRefNumLink">16</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit16" data-doi="10.1016/j.eml.2016.03.006"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Jia, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, T.</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Extreme Mech.         </div><div class="casAbstract">We report the creation of a nanoscale electrochem. device inside a transmission electron microscope-consisting of a single tin dioxide (SnO2) nanowire anode, an ionic liq. electrolyte, and a bulk lithium cobalt dioxide (LiCoO2) cathode-and the in situ observation of the lithiation of the SnO2 nanowire during electrochem. charging.  Upon charging, a reaction front propagated progressively along the nanowire, causing the nanowire to swell, elongate, and spiral.  The reaction front is a "Medusa zone" contg. a high d. of mobile dislocations, which are continuously nucleated and absorbed at the moving front.  This dislocation cloud indicates large in-plane misfit stresses and is a structural precursor to electrochem. driven solid-state amorphization.  Because lithiation-induced vol. expansion, plasticity, and pulverization of electrode materials are the major mech. effects that plague the performance and lifetime of high-capacity anodes in lithium-ion batteries, our observations provide important mechanistic insight for the design of advanced batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGr_6CVVGvaJ-bVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljhTS9uIvcbfA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3cXhsFaht7zM&amp;md5=5133171b13221aa2bd933f452e34f203</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref23"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref23" class="rightTabRefNumLink">23</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit23" data-doi="10.1016/j.carbon.2012.04.025"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liu, X.                         rightTab-cas_cit23R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">23</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">In situ transmission electron microscopy of electrochemical lithiation, delithiation and deformation of individual graphene nanoribbons</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Liu, Xiao Hua; Wang, Jiang Wei; Liu, Yang; Zheng, He; Kushima, Akihiro; Huang, Shan; Zhu, Ting; Mao, Scott X.; Li, Ju; Zhang, Sulin; Lu, Wei; Tour, James M.; Huang, Jian Yu</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Carbon</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2012</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">50</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">10</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3836-3844</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CRBNAH</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0008-6223</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">An in situ transmission electron microscopy study is reported of the electrochem. behavior of few-layer graphene nanoribbons (GNRs) synthesized by longitudinal splitting the multi-walled carbon nanotubes (MWCNTs).  Upon lithiation, the GNRs were covered by a nanocryst. lithium oxide layer attached to the surfaces and edges of the GNRs, most of which were removed upon delithiation, indicating that the lithiation/delithiation processes occurred predominantly at the surfaces of GNRs.  The lithiated GNRs were mech. robust during the tension and compression tests, in sharp contrast to the easy and brittle fracture of the lithiated MWCNTs.  This difference is attributed to the unconfined stacking of planar carbon layers in GNRs leading to a weak coupling between the intralayer and interlayer deformations, as opposed to the cylindrically confined carbon nanotubes where the interlayer lithium produces large tensile hoop stresses within the circumferentially-closed carbon layers, causing the ease of brittle fracture.  These results suggest substantial promise of graphene for building durable batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrhIoXomNzvvrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhEJu19iOEBnA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38Xmt12iur4%253D&amp;md5=75bec3f7b0901f47b840be8a9a784951</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref24"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref24" class="rightTabRefNumLink">24</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit24" data-doi="10.1021/nl402644w"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liang, W.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hong, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yang, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Fan, F.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liu, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Huang, J.                 10.1021/nl201684d</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit30R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">30</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Anisotropic Swelling and Fracture of Silicon Nanowires during Lithiation</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Liu, Xiao Hua; Zheng, He; Zhong, Li; Huang, Shan; Karki, Khim; Zhang, Li Qiang; Liu, Yang; Kushima, Akihiro; Liang, Wen Tao; Wang, Jiang Wei; Cho, Jeong-Hyun; Epstein, Eric; Dayeh, Shadi A.; Picraux, S. Tom; Zhu, Ting; Li, Ju; Sullivan, John P.; Cumings, John; Wang, Chunsheng; Mao, Scott X.; Ye, Zhi Zhen; Zhang, Sulin; Huang, Jian Yu</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Letters</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2011</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">11</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">8</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3312-3318</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NALEFD</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1530-6984</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The authors report direct observation of an unexpected anisotropic swelling of Si nanowires during lithiation against either a solid electrolyte with a Li counter-electrode or a liq. electrolyte with a LiCoO2 counter-electrode.  Such anisotropic expansion is attributed to the interfacial processes of accommodating large volumetric strains at the lithiation reaction front that depend sensitively on the crystallog. orientation.  This anisotropic swelling results in lithiated Si nanowires with a remarkable dumbbell-shaped cross section, which develops due to plastic flow and an ensuing necking instability that is induced by the tensile hoop stress buildup in the lithiated shell.  The plasticity-driven morphol. instabilities often lead to fracture in lithiated nanowires, now captured in video.  These results provide important insight into the battery degrdn. mechanisms.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGohhMVk4OC8MbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhEJu19iOEBnA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3MXotlGnsbo%253D&amp;md5=4c801363ba407d8179cba96d569b0dc5</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref31"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref31" class="rightTabRefNumLink">31</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit31" data-doi="10.1021/acsnano.5b01681"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Luo, L.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Silicon has been identified as a highly promising anode for next-generation lithium-ion batteries (LIBs).  The key challenge for Si anodes is large vol. change during the lithiation/delithiation cycle that results in chemomech. degrdn. and subsequent rapid capacity fading.  Here we report a novel fabrication method for hierarchically porous Si nanospheres (hp-SiNSs), which consist of a porous shell and a hollow core.  On charge/discharge cycling, the hp-SiNSs accommodate the vol. change through reversible inward Li breathing with negligible particle-level outward expansion.  Our mechanics anal. revealed that such inward expansion is enabled by the much stiffer lithiated layer than the unlithiated porous layer.  LIBs assembled with the hp-SiNSs exhibit high capacity, high power and long cycle life, which is superior to the current com. Si-based anode materials.  The low-cost synthesis approach provides a new avenue for the rational design of hierarchically porous structures with unique materials properties.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGo7mNP5yCcxv7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhEJu19iOEBnA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhslyht7fN&amp;md5=ec70cd6bfde00198bcc3e6ff9f9c6d2a</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref34"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref34" class="rightTabRefNumLink">34</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit34" data-doi="10.1038/nnano.2007.411"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Chan, C.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The rechargeable Li batteries with higher energy capacity and longer cycle life are necessary for application in portable electronic devices, elec. vehicles and implantable medical devices.  Si is an attractive anode material for Li batteries because it has a low discharge potential and the highest known theor. charge capacity - 4,200 mA-h/g.  Although this is &gt;10 times higher than existing graphite anodes and much larger than various nitride and oxide materials, Si anodes have limited applications because Si's vol. changes by 400% upon insertion and extn. of Li which results in pulverization and capacity fading.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Efficient mech. energy harvesters enable various wearable devices and auxiliary energy supply.  Here we report a novel class of mech. energy harvesters via stress-voltage coupling in electrochem. alloyed electrodes.  The device consists of two identical Li-alloyed Si as electrodes, sepd. by electrolyte-soaked polymer membranes.  Bending-induced asym. stresses generate chem. p.d., driving lithium ion flux from the compressed to the tensed electrode to generate elec. current. For the thin-film-based energy harvester used in this study, the device has achieved a generating capacity of 15%.                         rightTab-cas_cit42R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">42</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A chemo-mechanical model of lithiation in silicon</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yang, Hui; Fan, Feifei; Liang, Wentao; Guo, Xu; Zhu, Ting; Zhang, Sulin</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the Mechanics and Physics of Solids</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">We present a chemo-mech. model to investigate the lithiation-induced phase transformation, morphol. evolution, stress generation and fracture in cryst. silicon nanowires (c-SiNWs).  The model couples lithium (Li) diffusion with elasto-plastic deformation in a three-dimensional (3D) setting.  Several key features obsd. from recent transmission electron microscopy (TEM) studies are incorporated into the model, including the sharp interface between the lithiated amorphous shell and unlithiated cryst. core, crystallog. orientation dependent Li-Si reaction rate, and large-strain plasticity.  Our simulation results demonstrate that the model faithfully predicts the anisotropic swelling of lithiated SiNWs obsd. from previous exptl. studies.  Stress anal. from the finite-deformation model reveals that the SiNWs are prone to surface fracture at the angular sites where two adjacent {110} facets intersect, consistent with previous exptl. observations.  The mechanistic understanding of the morphol. evolution and stress generation sheds light on the design of failure-resistant nanostructured electrodes.  Our model offers a framework for the study of the chemo-mech. degrdn. in high-capacity electrode materials.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpV-GPEw3VAObVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liClTxupHdgLw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXhtlWrsrbN&amp;md5=71bf1f9d4aeafd2bd6cfaba577e628ed</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref43"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref43" class="rightTabRefNumLink">43</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit43" data-doi="10.1021/nn202071y"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liu, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zheng, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liu, X.                         rightTab-cas_cit48R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">48</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Two-Phase Electrochemical Lithiation in Amorphous Silicon</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Jiang Wei; He, Yu; Fan, Feifei; Liu, Xiao Hua; Xia, Shuman; Liu, Yang; Harris, C. Thomas; Li, Hong; Huang, Jian Yu; Mao, Scott X.; Zhu, Ting</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Letters</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">13</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">2</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">709-715</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Lithium-ion batteries have revolutionized portable electronics and will be a key to electrifying transport vehicles and delivering renewable electricity.  Amorphous silicon (a-Si) is being intensively studied as a high-capacity anode material for next-generation lithium-ion batteries.  Its lithiation has been widely thought to occur through a single-phase mechanism with gentle Li profiles, thus offering a significant potential for mitigating pulverization and capacity fade.  Here, a surprising two-phase process is discovered of electrochem. lithiation in a-Si by using in situ transmission electron microscopy.  The lithiation occurs by the movement of a sharp phase boundary between the a-Si reactant and an amorphous LixSi (a-LixSi, x â¼ 2.5) product.  Such a striking amorphous-amorphous interface exists until the remaining a-Si is consumed.  Then a second step of lithiation sets in without a visible interface, resulting in the final product of a-LixSi (x â¼ 3.75).  It is shown that the two-phase lithiation can be the fundamental mechanism underpinning the anomalous morphol. change of microfabricated a-Si electrodes, i.e., from a disk shape to a dome shape.  The results represent a significant step toward the understanding of the electrochem. driven reaction and degrdn. in amorphous materials, which is crit. to the development of microstructurally stable electrodes for high-performance lithium-ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGq03Vw14dqs0LVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liClTxupHdgLw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXoslOhtA%253D%253D&amp;md5=3438f8bafdffc05e0c09838a1cd7a88b</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref49"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref49" class="rightTabRefNumLink">49</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit49" data-doi="10.1021/nl3044508"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">McDowell, M. Mech. Phys.
